<span id="freeTextContainer11277445229117094317">"Red Sorghum" is one of the books I once started, but didn't finish, a book with multiple timelines that reach back to the time when Japan invaded China. The red in the title refers both to the Communist party, and to all the blood that was shed. I was at the Book Fair in Frankfurt when the Nobel Prize for Literature 2012 was announced: Mo Yan. <br/><br/>Reading into it again now, i remembered what i wrote about reading a previous Nobel Winner, Herta Müller: "war, and the hardship it brings on so many lev</span>
  <span id="freeText11277445229117094317" style="display:none">"Red Sorghum" is one of the books I once started, but didn't finish, a book with multiple timelines that reach back to the time when Japan invaded China. The red in the title refers both to the Communist party, and to all the blood that was shed. I was at the Book Fair in Frankfurt when the Nobel Prize for Literature 2012 was announced: Mo Yan. <br /><br />Reading into it again now, i remembered what i wrote about reading a previous Nobel Winner, Herta Müller: "war, and the hardship it brings on so many levels, the way it disrupts families, regions, futures. the approach of the book: history, seen through personal lives." - The same is true for Mo Yan. It's a hard, painful and complex read, spanning 3 generations, detailed in all aspects: rural life in China, and the hardship and cruelties the family has to go through.<br /><br />--<br />PS: this is a part of a longer book review that also includes some impressions from the book fair, and a second author from China: Liao Yiwu, who received one of the most important German book prizes at the book fair, and in counterpart to Mo Yan, had to flee his home country. here's the whole review: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://virtual-notes.blogspot.de/2012/10/words-wars-books-peace-prizes-time.html">http://virtual-notes.blogspot.de/2012...</a> </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer17088407866945235618"><br/>Where's the anger? This felt a little flat; needed more of a bite to it. The narrator seems to be suffering under an over-developed coping mechanism, passing off truly horrible shit with nice metaphors.   <span id="freeText17088407866945235618" style="display:none"><br />Where's the anger? This felt a little flat; needed more of a bite to it. The narrator seems to be suffering under an over-developed coping mechanism, passing off truly horrible shit with nice metaphors. Sometimes it felt a bit too cutesy-poo and a bit too conservative, "Those good old days of forced marriage and casual domestic brutality." Forced marriage is just wrong, Mo. It's not <b>only</b> a problem when she's beautiful and he has leprosy.<br /><br /><br /><br />"others got up to piss in a tin pail, raising a noisy liquid tattoo, like pearls falling onto a jade plate."<br /><br />"the crack of rifle fire, like the pop of a tautly stretched piece of silk pierced by a sharp knife."<br /><br />"a flow of lovely warm blood was released, soft and slippery, like the wing feathers of a bird..."<br /><br />"Uncle Arhat snagged the legs of [two dead bodies floating in a pond] – producing a sickening sound that made everyone's gums crawl, as though they had all bitten into sour apricots – then slowly dragged the bodies towards the bank."<br /><br />Great-Grandma and Great-Granddad: "looked like old toads fighting over a cicada."<br /><br />"What was at first a clean wound showing nothing more than a gleaming white piece of bone, gruesome and deathlike, soon began to spurt fresh blood, alternating between gushes and a slow ooze, droplets shining like so many strings of bright cherries."<br /><br />"A stumpy leg fell right in front of Mother, the white tendons all curled inward like river leeches."<br /><br />"Her lungs felt dry and chapped, each breath producing the rustling sound of withered sorghum leaves."<br /><br />"He squeezed the trigger, and the bullet hit the man right between the eyes, popping his green eyeballs out of their sockets like a pair of moth eggs." <br /><br />"Second Grandma felt a rush of pity for him , as she recognised his tortured look of self-loathing and shame under a thin layer of beaded sweat."</span>

<div id="review_475574867" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/475574867" />
    <a title="Alta" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2404463-alta"><img alt="Alta" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1419108364p2/2404463.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/475574867?book_show_action=true">Dec 11, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alta" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alta" href="/user/show/2404463-alta">Alta</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer475574867" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11060495782725367798"><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/894154.Red_Sorghum' title='Red Sorghum by Mo Yan' rel='nofollow noopener'>Red Sorghum</a> (Penguin Books, 1994) by <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/121407.Mo_Yan' title='Mo Yan' rel='nofollow noopener'>Mo Yan</a> is a historical novel that takes place in 1923-24 and the late thirties, during the war against Japan. Made into a very successful film by Jiang Yimou, Red Sorghum is packed with action, killings, guns, policemen, backstabbing, survival after near-death experiences, deeds of heroism and betrayal, and images of "lush" landscapes (the kind of landscapes that big Hollywood producers like). The novel is set in the village where Mo Yan grew up and which, af</span>
  <span id="freeText11060495782725367798" style="display:none"><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/894154.Red_Sorghum" title="Red Sorghum by Mo Yan" rel="nofollow noopener">Red Sorghum</a> (Penguin Books, 1994) by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/121407.Mo_Yan" title="Mo Yan" rel="nofollow noopener">Mo Yan</a> is a historical novel that takes place in 1923-24 and the late thirties, during the war against Japan. Made into a very successful film by Jiang Yimou, Red Sorghum is packed with action, killings, guns, policemen, backstabbing, survival after near-death experiences, deeds of heroism and betrayal, and images of "lush" landscapes (the kind of landscapes that big Hollywood producers like). The novel is set in the village where Mo Yan grew up and which, after he was awarded the Nobel, the Chinese government decided to proudly turn into a theme park. The setting is described with the passionate intensity of the former peasant (i.e., Mo Yan) who longs for his roots: “A boundless expanse of sorghum greeted the reddening sun”; “The space between heaven and earth was filled with the red dust of sorghum and the fragrance of sorghum wine”; “She opens her eyes amid the pearly drops of sorghum”; “Grandma and Granddad exchanged their love surrounded by the vitality of the sorghum field. … My father was conceived with the essence of heaven and earth…”; “[The sorghum stalks] begin to moan, to writhe, to shout, to entwine her.”<br /><br />Mo Yan is famous for his visceral naturalism, in which he presents the nature of all living creatures in a non-sentimental, raw way (his men fart and belch even in the most romantic moments). Here is an extreme example: “the bloated carcasses of dozens of mules had been found floating in the Black Water River …; their distended bellies, baked by the sun, split and popped, released their splendid innards, like gorgeous blooming flowers.” These descriptions are certainly beautiful, but when the author estheticizes scenes with people killed in horrible acts of violence, something seems wrong.<br /><br />Red Sorghum starts in 1939, when the narrator’s grandmother, a beautiful young woman of unusual intelligence and courage, is killed by the Japanese while bringing food to the father of her child, Yu Zhan’ao (also referred to as “Commander Yu”), and his comrades-in-arms. From here, the story goes backward, following a hard-to-define temporal structure that is also present in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/251392.The_Garlic_Ballads" title="The Garlic Ballads by Mo Yan" rel="nofollow noopener">The Garlic Ballads</a>. It would be too simplistic to say that the story moves back and forth in time, though it does move between 1939 and 1923 (before Douguan, the narrator’s father, was conceived), with occasional flashbacks or “flashforwards” to other years, one of them being 1985, when the narrator returns to his native village.<br /><br />In 1923 the narrator’s (future) grandmother is sixteen and betrothed to a rich man, owner of a sorghum wine distillery, who has only a small problem: he’s a leper. The future bride is transported in a sedan chair pulled by several hired men, including Yu Zhan’ao, who eventually kills the bridegroom and moves in with the bride, with whom he fathers a child, Douguan. The novel is written mainly in the third person, from the point of view of the narrator, who has heard the story from his father (Douguan), but there are moments when the author moves away from this structure to give a voice to other characters.<br />I confess I didn’t understand whether the fighting with the Japanese described for big chunks of the novel, had occurred only once, twice, or three or more times. The present is always mixed with the past (something specific to many Chinese writers) and the same butchery occurs in all the fighting scenes, so, in the end, it’s impossible to tell when a battle has ended and another one has begun. It all seems like the same, unending battle, to which the writer keeps coming back. Red Sorghum includes some of the most violent scenes I’ve ever encountered in a novel: a scene in which the Japanese order a local butcher to skin a man alive; a scene in which, after murdering with a bayonet a little girl in front of her mother, the Japanese soldiers rape and kill the latter; a scene in which, after a (or several) battle(s) with the Japanese, hundreds of dogs from the neighboring villages come to devour the corpses—a truly nightmarish landscape that proves the author’s penchant for horrific exaggeration.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11060495782725367798" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_42404617" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/42404617" />
    <a title="Zartor" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1884839-zartor"><img alt="Zartor" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/42404617?book_show_action=true">Jan 08, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Zartor" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Zartor" href="/user/show/1884839-zartor">Zartor</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer42404617" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7988877355385231280">Oh my hell, this book is so good! I'm on a roll lately! It's hard to explain...as he does in half of Republic of Wine, he uses a narrator far removed from actual events to tell the story, in this case the grandchild of one of the novel's protagonists. The story is ostensibly about all the things that went into making the narrator's father who he is, but it's pretty moving. A lot of the reality's of mid-20th century china blend in with fantastic story-telling, action, and extremely vivid imagery.</span>
  <span id="freeText7988877355385231280" style="display:none">Oh my hell, this book is so good! I'm on a roll lately! It's hard to explain...as he does in half of Republic of Wine, he uses a narrator far removed from actual events to tell the story, in this case the grandchild of one of the novel's protagonists. The story is ostensibly about all the things that went into making the narrator's father who he is, but it's pretty moving. A lot of the reality's of mid-20th century china blend in with fantastic story-telling, action, and extremely vivid imagery.<br /><br />Also, if you like to see (read) people die, this is the book for you. I think at least 2 characters died each chapter, in some cases dozens. If they didn't die, then they were permanently messed up. Yay war!</span>

<div id="review_650997362" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/650997362" />
    <a title="Jo" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12091097-jo"><img alt="Jo" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1346006680p2/12091097.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/650997362?book_show_action=true">Jun 25, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jo" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jo" href="/user/show/12091097-jo">Jo</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099451679" href="/book/show/472274.Red_Sorghum">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer650997362" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2969087127502945472">Mo Yan has an amazing way with words and description, this book is so dense with imagery that each page requires careful reading as you struggle to adequately create pictures in your mind from the language. It soon becomes clear why the book is called 'Red Sorghum' with reference to it in every chapter and the dark rich soil in which it grows, there is also a consistency in the imagery of the colours red and green tying together the sometimes confusing array of fights and battles that take place</span>
  <span id="freeText2969087127502945472" style="display:none">Mo Yan has an amazing way with words and description, this book is so dense with imagery that each page requires careful reading as you struggle to adequately create pictures in your mind from the language. It soon becomes clear why the book is called 'Red Sorghum' with reference to it in every chapter and the dark rich soil in which it grows, there is also a consistency in the imagery of the colours red and green tying together the sometimes confusing array of fights and battles that take place. Red is the colour not only of the sorghum but of the myriad amount of blood that is spilled throughout the book and that adds rather gruesomely to the richness of the soil; this is not a book to be read by the faint of heart. <br /><br />The book moves around a lot between a span of about fifteen years or so and many times when a new chapter started I was lost as to which time period we were being told about and how that related to the text that came before. The way in which the main characters are referred to by Grandad, Father and Grandma can also be confusing when we know so very little about the narrator but these few points of confusion are my only criticism and could be due to the reader not the writer. <br /><br />When writing about his characters, there are no subtle emotions; they are ruled by love, desire, anger, fear and hatred but then in times of war and in a place where it is a struggle to survive there is no room for ambiguity or siting on a fence. This is an epic tale with Yu Zhan'ao as the epic and flawed hero but it also has an epic cast of secondary characters- it would be hard to call them minor. Uncle Arhat, Pocky Leng, Passion, Black Eye, all play small but pivotal parts, even the animals seem integral to the story but it is the steadfast sorghum and the red sun, the Black water river and the ever shifting light that truly make this such a magical, resonant, timeless story.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2969087127502945472" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_496489136" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/496489136" />
    <a title="Stephen Durrant" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1457001-stephen-durrant"><img alt="Stephen Durrant" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1362701086p2/1457001.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/496489136?book_show_action=true">Jan 05, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephen Durrant" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephen Durrant" href="/user/show/1457001-stephen-durrant">Stephen Durrant</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer496489136" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer552952608232306206">Mo Yan's novels are often praised because they depict the history of China's last six or so decades from the ground up. That is, they are usually set in the countryside, specifically his native Gaomi Township in Shandong, and concern precisely that class of people the Revolution was supposed to liberate. In the case of Red Sorghum, a 1987-novel that Zhang Yimou famously turned into a film, the historical background is the resistance against Japanese occupiers. Mo Yan's ground-up depiction of thi</span>
  <span id="freeText552952608232306206" style="display:none">Mo Yan's novels are often praised because they depict the history of China's last six or so decades from the ground up. That is, they are usually set in the countryside, specifically his native Gaomi Township in Shandong, and concern precisely that class of people the Revolution was supposed to liberate. In the case of Red Sorghum, a 1987-novel that Zhang Yimou famously turned into a film, the historical background is the resistance against Japanese occupiers. Mo Yan's ground-up depiction of this period, far from being a story of heroic resistance, shows us a confused world of inter-bandit and inter-region rivalries that are fighting each other as much as the Japanese invaders. There is genuine heroism, to be sure, but it takes place much more against a backdrop of revenge than any ideal, nationalist or otherwise. Well, the world, at least on the ground level, is always messy and this novel captures that well. Like other of Mo Yan's works, and so many modern Chinese novels, the story is intergenerational. The narrator tells of his grandfather, largely as he was seen through the eyes of his father. That grandfather is by turns a ruthless killer, a kindly father, and a courageous bandit-military leader. His bande-dessinée-like quality can be excused in part by the fact that he is double memory, a son's memory of his father's memory. As always in Mo Yan, there is plenty of the blood, guts, gore, and slime of physical life that this novelist looks at so unflinchingly . . . and sometimes obsessively. But along with that one sometimes encounters very lyrical, even magical, descriptions of the countryside--in this case the fields of red sorghum. This novel is a good place to begin in reading Mo Yan, especially when it is followed with the film, but I prefer his later "Life and Death is Wearing me Out."</span>
  <a data-text-id="552952608232306206" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1143577385" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1143577385" />
    <a title="Gisela Hafezparast" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/24952902-gisela-hafezparast"><img alt="Gisela Hafezparast" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1382532557p2/24952902.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1143577385?book_show_action=true">Dec 27, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gisela Hafezparast" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gisela Hafezparast" href="/user/show/24952902-gisela-hafezparast">Gisela Hafezparast</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24952902-gisela-hafezparast?shelf=i-finally-get-my-turn-bookshelf">i-finally-get-my-turn-bookshelf</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1143577385" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17096878225326296582">I cannot believe I have let this book sit on my bookshelves unread for the past 3 years or so. The reason of course is that from the first few pages it feels very much like a war book, which it is, but it is so much more. It is an epic tale about the Chinese civil war and the Japanese invasion of China and the heroic resistance of the Chinese. I have rarely read a tale so beautiful and sympathetically told which in the main is mostly about murder, slaughter of the innocent and those not so innoc</span>
  <span id="freeText17096878225326296582" style="display:none">I cannot believe I have let this book sit on my bookshelves unread for the past 3 years or so. The reason of course is that from the first few pages it feels very much like a war book, which it is, but it is so much more. It is an epic tale about the Chinese civil war and the Japanese invasion of China and the heroic resistance of the Chinese. I have rarely read a tale so beautiful and sympathetically told which in the main is mostly about murder, slaughter of the innocent and those not so innocent, together with a very complicated love affair. When reading so suffer, love, rejoice and hate with all the characters, but at the end there are really very few of the main characters which when considered can be said to be "good people". Especially the two main characters, one a bandit and war load, the other a strong but very calculating women (although at least she either had to learn to make her own decisions or be sacrificed) I will have to think about for quite a while until I decide where I stand with regard to them. Their son (father) is the only one, although also after a while an accomplished fighter, is the easiest to be sympathised most. It's a tale of old China, although I had to keep telling myself this was the time roughly around the second world war, but I suspect it could have been anytime during China's long history. The cruelty of poor people's life is breathtaking and sometimes feels like you are being dunked into ice-cold water, it is so chilling. And then they get up again, start again - amazing. <br /><br />This book is in line with War and Peace and I will certainly read more of Mo Yan's books. If they are even half as good as this one, he is a very deserving Nobel Prize winner for Literatue.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17096878225326296582" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Suresh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Suresh" href="/user/show/10542445-suresh">Suresh</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer14503495640667110483">This book tells a tale spanning three generations in Northeast Gaomi township in Shandong province China. There is a marked contrast between the beauty of the land and the sorghum fields and the depravity and darkness in the actual events which transpire. The narrative is bewildering in that it jumps around in time almost paragraph to paragraph and it makes for having to reread earlier sections to reconnect the storyline. No character is completely one way or another. Like the contrasts set out </span>
  <span id="freeText14503495640667110483" style="display:none">This book tells a tale spanning three generations in Northeast Gaomi township in Shandong province China. There is a marked contrast between the beauty of the land and the sorghum fields and the depravity and darkness in the actual events which transpire. The narrative is bewildering in that it jumps around in time almost paragraph to paragraph and it makes for having to reread earlier sections to reconnect the storyline. No character is completely one way or another. Like the contrasts set out by Yan Mo in the beginning, everyone has heroic and bastardly attributes. That's part of what makes the book so authentic and really brings to life the horror of the Sino-Japanese war. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14503495640667110483" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_491008809" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/491008809" />
    <a title="Hock Tjoa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4705351-hock-tjoa"><img alt="Hock Tjoa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1464976177p2/4705351.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/491008809?book_show_action=true">Dec 31, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hock Tjoa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hock Tjoa" href="/user/show/4705351-hock-tjoa">Hock Tjoa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer491008809" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4539637445641974804">This is a bold, brash, bawdy and brilliant work. Purporting to be a family chronicle that the narrator obtains from a 92 year old woman from his family village, it tells of his brave, lusty and larger than life grandparents and their turbulent existence carving out a life in the midst of a northern Chinese region infested with bandits, opium smokers and gamblers, and invaded by the Japanese. There are horrific scenes of brutality: second grandma (thereon hangs another tale) and young auntie were</span>
  <span id="freeText4539637445641974804" style="display:none">This is a bold, brash, bawdy and brilliant work. Purporting to be a family chronicle that the narrator obtains from a 92 year old woman from his family village, it tells of his brave, lusty and larger than life grandparents and their turbulent existence carving out a life in the midst of a northern Chinese region infested with bandits, opium smokers and gamblers, and invaded by the Japanese. There are horrific scenes of brutality: second grandma (thereon hangs another tale) and young auntie were raped and mutilated, Uncle Arhat was skinned alive, Grandpa himself murdered Grandma's newly wed husband and father-in-law (you simply have to read this book).<br /><br />The author paints his scenes in vivid language: the red sorghum of the title itself refers to the hardy grain that shimmered in fields like a "sea of blood"; dancing, it "reeked of glory; cold and graceful, it promised enchantment; passionate and loving, it was tumultuous." The narrator felt a pang on a return visit, finding the fields planted with a hybrid variety of sorghum that never seemed to ripen; he was haunted by "a nagging sense of our species' regression."<br /><br />Though Grandma died in the main battle told, that of the Black Water River with several bands of Chinese ill-coordinated against the Japanese, she was the heroine--lovely as foot-binding reduced her feet to three inch "lotuses" so that when she walked, "her body swayed like a willow in the wind," and as opium-smoking short of addiction had given her "the complexion of a peach, a sunny disposition and a clear mind." She died unafraid but unwillingly; unafraid of the eighteen levels of Hell but unwilling to let go of life.<br /><br />The novel covers much more--a digression into the marauding packs of dogs almost has the dogs taking on the voice of the narrator; a survivor of the war who lives on into the middle of the 20th century, Old Eighteen Stabs Geng who lived despite those wounds because of the magical ministrations of a silver fox, succumbs to starvation when his pension is held up by a petty bureaucrat.<br /> <br />Should the author have put more energy into decrying such evil? This reviewer will not judge, only saying this--that it would have been criminal not to have written this book or to have published it. The Nobel was well earned.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4539637445641974804" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2278561664" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2278561664" />
    <a title="TylerGHS2020" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/71701743-tylerghs2020"><img alt="TylerGHS2020" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/images/nophoto/user/c_111x148.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2278561664?book_show_action=true">Apr 05, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="TylerGHS2020" class="user" itemprop="url" name="TylerGHS2020" href="/user/show/71701743-tylerghs2020">TylerGHS2020</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2278561664" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11431160270745115501">As someone who's typically interested in war-related books, this didn't live up to my expectations. It is a fictional history/war book, but still seemed to portray the typical themes of war rather strongly and vividly. I was expecting a more fast-paced read, but this book didn't follow that style. A lot of the book is related to symbolism. The narrator helps portray the realistic brutalities of war, which seems to be one of the primary themes of the book. The part the plant plays on the novel se</span>
  <span id="freeText11431160270745115501" style="display:none">As someone who's typically interested in war-related books, this didn't live up to my expectations. It is a fictional history/war book, but still seemed to portray the typical themes of war rather strongly and vividly. I was expecting a more fast-paced read, but this book didn't follow that style. A lot of the book is related to symbolism. The narrator helps portray the realistic brutalities of war, which seems to be one of the primary themes of the book. The part the plant plays on the novel seems to be that even through years of war and bloodshed, it still provides food, shelter, etc. There's also some realistic and brutal imagery, which helps convey the themes of this book. Something I found interesting was how the main characters and their opponents aren't fighting for a political party or something of that type, but for their land. And all the struggles the people have are witnessed by the sorghum plants, as they're stained with blood, hence the name "<i>Red</i> Sorghum". This novel is definitely fueled by symbolism rather than suspense or action. I found it rather boring due to that, but it adds a very interesting factor to the book. I gave this book a 3/5 because what it lacks in action and suspense, it compensates somewhat with symbolism and reality. Whether you like this book or not is based completely on personal preference, so if you like fast-paced, suspenseful novels, I wouldn't recommend this. If you like realistic, brutal, symbolic type novels, I would recommend it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11431160270745115501" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_533230097" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/533230097" />
    <a title="Dan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11015938-dan"><img alt="Dan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442950126p2/11015938.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/533230097?book_show_action=true">Feb 11, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dan" href="/user/show/11015938-dan">Dan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer533230097" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8334366760491046651">With all the controversy surrounding Mo Yan's receipt of the Nobel Prize in Literature last year I thought it might be interesting to read something of what he's written. Overall, I enjoyed the book (or really,s series of books within one cover), with the interconnected stories of different family members and various tangential characters. It's the first time I've read a Chinese novel, and I don't know how much is the translation and how much the original, but I did find the constant back and fo</span>
  <span id="freeText8334366760491046651" style="display:none">With all the controversy surrounding Mo Yan's receipt of the Nobel Prize in Literature last year I thought it might be interesting to read something of what he's written. Overall, I enjoyed the book (or really,s series of books within one cover), with the interconnected stories of different family members and various tangential characters. It's the first time I've read a Chinese novel, and I don't know how much is the translation and how much the original, but I did find the constant back and forth to different parts of the timeline, generally with no indication that a time shift was taking place, to be a little disconcerting. I often found myself a paragraph or two into a section before realizing that the story had jumped 20 years in one direction or another. And, while I realize that the title of the book involves the plant red sorghum, obviously, there are whole sections of the book where the phrase is used in every sentence, page after page, relentlessly. We got it, red sorghum is important to the story. You don't have to beat us over the head with it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8334366760491046651" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789187179174" href="/book/show/16125534-det-r-da-f-ltet">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5935491-tanja-berg?shelf=audio">audio</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5935491-tanja-berg?shelf=wish-i-had-not-read">wish-i-had-not-read</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1689752275" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12139811883582008416">Well-written, haunting, plenty of new perspectives. Except I hated it. I would never ever ever have finished it had not been on audio. I have acquainted myself with the depth of human evil in this book. Atrocities are described in great detail. Usually such books give you some little thing to cling to, some flowering plant in the desolation of human waste. Not so here. There is only misery, survival for some and death for most.<br/><br/>The narrator is the grand child of one of the main characters. Most o</span>
  <span id="freeText12139811883582008416" style="display:none">Well-written, haunting, plenty of new perspectives. Except I hated it. I would never ever ever have finished it had not been on audio. I have acquainted myself with the depth of human evil in this book. Atrocities are described in great detail. Usually such books give you some little thing to cling to, some flowering plant in the desolation of human waste. Not so here. There is only misery, survival for some and death for most.<br /><br />The narrator is the grand child of one of the main characters. Most of the book is about the Japanese invasion during the second world war. There is a separation of time lines which was occasionally confusing since I had it on audio. After a while I did not care anymore. If this is all we are, monsters, we may as well go and kill ourselves now, and spare the future of our maggotridden souls. But I suppose war brings out the worst in people, the author says as much. Frankly, I would rather not dwell on it. Although I have an affinity for horror, there is only so much torture, blood and entrails I can stand. This book gave me enough of it to last a lifetime.</span>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Ze6sDtawgpujbCbMawpAsBnHWmtUEPjTIzyt6sTGenL4yhgTPG57VhFEHtiWUWfSg9KqF/TG51eO2g4ChXymBw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Lhc+8mqVw4M+7CTlK+7RiLrKGMUGwWY5CJlIkKEdGYmzM4rvgEs6TozEHPHWtfbqIN/ouaYXeb2lf+t44KfF/A==')})
  }

In “Disparity,” for example, Yu Hua illustrates the mind-boggling economic gaps that separate citizens of the country. In “Copycat,” he depicts the escalating trend of piracy and imitation as a creative new form of revolutionary action. And in “Bamboozle,” he describes the increasingly brazen practices of trickery, fraud, and chicanery that are, he suggests, becoming a way of life at every level of society. Characterized by Yu Hua’s trademark wit, insight, and courage, China in Ten Words is a refreshingly candid vision of the “Chinese miracle” and all its consequences, from the singularly invaluable perspective of a writer living Our attachment to the vita activa creates an imperative to work which degrades the human being into a labouring animal, an animal laborans. At the same time, the hyperactivity which characterizes our daily routines robs human beings of the capacity to linger and the faculty of contemplation. It therefore becomes impossible to experience time as fulfilling.\n\nDrawing on a range of thinkers including Heidegger, Nietzsche and Arendt, Han argues that we can overcome this temporal crisis only by revitalizing the vita contemplativa and relearning the art of lingering. For what distinguishes humans from other animals is the capacity for reflection and contemplation, and when life regains this capacity, this art of lingering, it gains in time and space, in duration       var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_120009'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/120009.The_Basil_and_Josephine_Stories?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">The Basil and Josephine Stories<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/3190.F_Scott_Fitzgerald?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">F. Scott Fitzgerald<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.95 avg rating &mdash; 746 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1928\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer2856247072647660253\">\n  Fourteen of F. Scott Fitzgerald\'s best-loved and most beguiling stories, together in a single volume\n  In 1928, while struggling with his novel Tender Is the Night, Fitzgerald began writing a series of stories about Basil Duke Lee, a fictionalized<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText2856247072647660253\" style=\"display:none\">\n  Fourteen of F. Scott Fitzgerald\'s best-loved and most beguiling stories, together in a single volume\n  In 1928, while struggling with his novel Tender Is the Night, Fitzgerald began writing a series of stories about Basil Duke Lee, a fictionalized version of his younger self. Drawing on his childhood and adolescent experiences, Fitzgerald wrote nine tales that were published in the Saturday Evening Post about his life from the time he was an eleven-year-old boy living in Buffalo, New York, until he entered Princeton University in 1913. Then from 1930 to 1931, with Tender Is the Night still unfinished, Fitzgerald wrote five more stories (also published in the Post) that centered around Josephine Perry, Basil\'s female counterpart. Although Fitzgerald intended to combine the fourteen Basil Lee and Josephine Perry stories into a single work, he never succeeded in doing so in his lifetime. Here, The Basil and Josephine Stories brings together in one volume the complete set, resulting in one of Fitzgerald\'s most charming and evocative works.<\/span>\n  <a Set in a poor village in Henan province, it is a <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText4428150451977729896\" style=\"display:none\">Officially censored upon its Chinese publication, and the subject of a bitter lawsuit between author and publisher, Dream of Ding Village is Chinese novelist Yan Lianke\'s most important novel to date. Set in a poor village in Henan province, it is a deeply moving and beautifully written account of a blood-selling ring in contemporary China. Based on a real-life blood-selling scandal in eastern China, Dream of Ding Village is the result of three years of undercover work by Yan Lianke, who worked as an assistant to a well-known Beijing anthropologist in an effort to study a small village decimated by HIV/AIDS as a result of unregulated blood selling. Whole villages were wiped out with no responsibility taken or reparations paid. Dream of Ding Village focuses on one family, destroyed when one son rises to the top of the Party pile as he exploits the situation, while another son is infected and dies. The result is a passionate and steely critique of the rate at which China is developing and what happens to those who get in the way.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"4428150451977729896\" href=\"#\"           <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer8095777423523105965">Modern Chinese author, in the western world most known for his novel <i>Red Sorghum</i> (which was turned into a movie by the same title). Often described as the Chinese Franz Kafka or Joseph Heller.<br/><br/><i>Mo Yan</i> (莫言) is a pen name and means <i>don't speak</i>. His real name is Guan Moye (simplified Chinese: 管谟业; traditional Chinese: 管謨業; pinyin: Guǎn Móyè).<br/><br/>He has been awarded the Nobel Prize in Literature 2012 for hi</span>
  <span id="freeText8095777423523105965" style="display:none">Modern Chinese author, in the western world most known for his novel <i>Red Sorghum</i> (which was turned into a movie by the same title). Often described as the Chinese Franz Kafka or Joseph Heller.<br /><br /><i>Mo Yan</i> (莫言) is a pen name and means <i>don't speak</i>. His real name is Guan Moye (simplified Chinese: 管谟业; traditional Chinese: 管謨業; pinyin: Guǎn Móyè).<br /><br />He has been awarded the Nobel Prize in Literature 2012 for his work which "with hallucinatory realism merges folk tales, history and the contemporary". Among the works highlighted by the Nobel judges were <i>Red Sorghum</i> (1987) and <i>Big Breasts &amp; Wide Hips</i> (2004), as well as <i>The Garlic Ballads</i>.<br /><br />Chinese version: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/20094491.__" title="莫言" rel="nofollow noopener">莫言</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="8095777423523105965" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
